<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



You've been given some seriously good advice already.  You're not going to be able to keep this a secret so you might as well come clean as advised.  I would tell them that the school was all booked up and that I will be going for a full time job.  I can't figure out what insurance has to do with anything where going to school is concerned unless she is referring to a college annuity or something.  From the sounds of things, if they don't boot you out they will likely expect you to pay some sort of rent towards your upkeep which would have otherwise been going to the college costs.  Working could open other doors of opportunity for you.  Many businesses of any size, especially government, have financial assistance programs in place that could help you with your college education and relieve your parents of some of the financial burdens that go along with putting kids through college.  In fact, that could be a point of persuasion for your parents to let you do your thing.  In the meantime, prepare to go to college.  The income betterment can make the difference between a successful life and barely making ends meet.



</answer>

Been There~Done That!
Member since: 23 May 2006

Contributing In:

<answer>



Alcoholism is a disease.



It is not always how much you drink or how often that puts you in the category.

My father was an alcoholic. He learned to drink with the best, the Marines! It got to a point where it was devastating to us and him as well. We as a family put our foot down and drove him to an Outpatient Rehabilitation Center. Humbled a bit he accepted the help and did well. He even went to work in a Young Adult Alcohol Rehab Unit for a year afterwards. The kid's loved him and looked up to him because he was a retired Marine and he was not too proud to admit to them his faults. The sad part of this is that my mother never let him live it down and at home he was what you call a "dry drunk". He never drank again even if there were times he wished he could have.



If the concern is there in you then it should be a concern with your mother and she is the one that needs to step up and let him know her limitations with it and try to get him to seek help.



You can get in with an Al-Anon Group n your area and learn more about how to deal with this. There are good ones and bad ones, big picky about it. When we as a family went I stopped going because all they wanted to do was talk about the bad things their family member did drinking. Nothing positive was ever mentioned about the fact they were making their life better for being a part of rehab. I chose to look forward and not backwards. I wish my mother had done the same.

Alcoholism is a disease and you get to a point that it is not so much a choice of the one drinking it is something their body craves and the crave overtakes what is right or wrong. They loose control, just like smoking.



Again, work together as a family. Instead of confronting your father at this point confront your mother and other siblings that may be too use to living with it and dealing with it only to wake up the next day and deal again. There has to be a stopping point and it will either be his health that does it or a fmaily that cares enough to not live around it.



</answer>

Well, for a start, he is obviously an alcoholic and is damaging his own health anyway  - and certainly not helping any health problems he already has.
It`s a known fact that alcoholics can only stop drinking themselves.  They really have to want to.
I think all you can do is mention it to him occasionally  - and point out that it will finally end in an early and unpleasant death.  But it`s no use nagging, as that will only make it worse.
Sorry about this worrying thing in your life - but it is happening in hundreds if not thousands of homes.



Alcoholics are selfish people: it`s a free country to do whatever we want within the law and as long as it is not hurting or impacting on others...but alcoholism  does impact on others. Very much so.



</answer>

Douglas D

Total points: 21805 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



OK let's get something straight right at the start. While you prefer to describe your dad as a "problem drinker", by definition he is an ALCOHOLIC!  Anyone who has problems in his/her life due to an addiction such as alcohol, recognizes that there are problems, but continues to abuse their substance of choice is an addict, & your dad's addiction is to alcohol, so that makes him an alcoholic OK?

Once it was established that your dad is an alcoholic, all of that other stuff you wrote about (while very relevant) didn't need to be said, since your story is very typical of a child of an alcoholic. 
You could very well write down your entire life story with your father from your earliest memory all the way to the present, & it could easily end up being a word for word description, & life manual for what life is like living with an alcoholic in your family.

Since you are now a young adult, & have more options & choices open to you then you did when you were a small child forced to live under the same roof as an alcoholic, then my advice to you is to turn your focus from HIM, to YOU.
I know that many of us are raised with the belief that it is our burden from birth to protect our family members from all perils, even when it risks our own safety & well-being. But there needs to be limits to that, especially when the family member in harms way seems to be so determined to self-destruct.

That is why I know that you could be helped tremedously by meeting with others, & learning how they have coped with their lives with their addicts. So don't be shy, contact your nearest chapter of ALANON, (they may be closer than you think).

Meanwhile I highly, highly recommend that you read a book by Melodie Beatty titled "Co-Dependant No More".
It helped me, & I KNOW it will help you to read it as well, so find a copy. Start with the library if you want, but I'll bet they have trouble keeping their copy available because, as I said, your story is not unusual so therefore you are not alone.

Good Luck!



</answer>

<answer>



based on experience, i can tell you that unfortunately, your dad is only going to get help when he sees that he needs it. nothing you, or anyone else, can say will make him get help until he realizes that he has a problem.

i am sure you already know this, but alcoholics see drinking as a solution, not as the problem. sometimes it takes losing everything to realize what they had.

you should move out if that's what you want to do, and you should not feel guilty about leaving your dad to deal with his problem. in fact, it would probably help him.

if you have other family members that are also affected by this, you could try something like an "intervention". in my situation, we did that, and it actually got a little worse before it got better. but you kind of have to expect that. people with drinking problems will get offended when they are approached about it, and they will feel like they are being attacked. i am not sure if this has ever happened to you, but for me, whenever we tried to approach the person, they would place the blame on us, in order to take the attention off of themselves. that is a form of denial... making you feel guilty for their problems.

to summarize everything i just said, i think you should move out and move on with your life. you won't be affected as much that way as you are now, with living with it. you may be worried about your father, but like i said, this is something he is going to have to get through himself. unfortunately, it is hard, but it will all come in time.

you could try mentioning getting help for him, but he may become angry with you, since he doesn't see it as a problem.

i really wish you the best, and i hope you can get through this in a healthy way. i know drinking problems affect everyone around the person, and i think the best thing for you to do is create a life for yourself, and stay away from that type of environment.

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcoholics_...



</answer>

<answer>



I don't really understand why you've stayed there until you are 28. Maybe you feel some misplaced sense of responsibility for your father. The fact is that he is the one who was (until you were 18, at least) supposed to be responsible for you, not the other way around. You do not need to feel, or be, responsible for him or trying to save him or look after him.
He has chosen the life that he leads. Everyone, including alcoholics, make their choices, and he has made his. If he had chosen a different path, he could have put caring for his family first and got help from AA or some similar organisation. He chose not to do that, so don't hold yourself responsible for taking care of him. 
Get out now and make a life for yourself. It's totally your choice whether you even stay in contact with him. As things currently stand, it sounds as though you are enabling him more than anything else. Most alcoholics have to hit rock bottom before they will get help, and you are right that problems don't always just get neatly sorted out. He may never give up drinking, and if he doesn't, that is 100% his own choice. He can choose to get help at any time. 
Anyway, go and live your own life to the full. Join a support group for the families of alcoholics. Al-Anon is one, and they will be able to help you put things in perspective.
Good luck.



</answer>

<answer>



It sounds like you've been resigned to it for a long time, which shows some maturity on your part.

I do feel sad for your mother though, it's possible she still loves your dad and wants to keep trying, but it's hard when the other person really doesn't want to.



I think the best thing to do for your mother is to encourage her to keep busy and active, so that she is at least tired enough to sleep at night - sleep is a great healer of both body and spirit. The last thing she needs is to have other men in her life right now, so don't try to encourage her to date or whatever. Much better that she makes new friends instead, particularly people in similar situations.



</answer>

Physical (corporal) punishment is inappropriate at any age and under any circumstances and is usually the recourse of people with little imagination or who have been brought up themselves with beatings. <answer>



When I found out that I had to make this speech I found an agent who could get in touch with the word's most glamorous women so that at long last my mother could join the firmament of the stars. My idea was a necklace where she would be the chief diadem. Harry, the agent, called around, got Kirsten Dunst on the phone, and asked her, as a special favor--he also offered $2 million--to be here, but when she heard that my mother was going to be present too, she couldn't stand the competition, so we had to nix her. Then after that we tried Angelina Jolie. She was hard to reach, in France. She considered the idea, but became very evasive when she heard why Dunst had said no.

In the next three weeks, we ran through a roster of Hollywood, everyone from Eva Longoria to Beyonce, and Paris Hilton to Kate Olsen, Reese Witherspoon to Drew Barrymore. Only Phyllis Diller said that she would come, but I don't think _I_ could stand that. We had no shortage of male applicants, including Brad Pitt, Matt Damon (he's getting kind of heavy, though), George Clooney, and the Jonas Brothers--but we are not trying to set my mother up: we're trying to set her off.

So there she is, a rose in a field yarrow, a dahlia in a field of clover, Botticelli's inspiration and despair, the original for Helen of Troy, Eve, Juliet, Cleopatra, Ishtar, Daisy Buchanan, and the girl in the car in _American Grafitti._ Fall upon the thorns of life, ye proud, and bleed!



</answer>

Orla C
Member since: 02 August 2006
Total points: 31233 (Level 7)

<answer>



If you're boss is such a bully, complain to HER boss. Have a list of the times she bullied you. If her boss does nothing about it, go to your local citizen's information bureau or equivalent and ask them what to do about this.



I'm afraid unless you can get someone to back you up, or there is a record of this somewhere, it could boil down to your word against hers.



I would go back to the place where you did your course, and tell them that you are really serious about finding another job, and could they give you a letter of recommendation, something like that, that you can present to potential employers. Ask them if they know of any vacancies that you can fill in, even if these are temporary. And go to temping agencies also. Work on your look, most of these places like their temps to look professional. Don't be afraid to ask how you can improve yourself in ways other than the actual job.



You could also ask your current boss' boss if there are vacancies in other departments, so you don't have to deal with your current boss in the same way. Real mud in her eye if you end up working at a higher end than she is now! Revenge is a dish best served cold ....



</answer>

<question>



Older generation - how do you cope being the older generation.  I mean without your folks, other loved ones ?



etc?  I am 32 and today I am just having a really lousy day (not like me normally I am so cheery!) and as daft as it sounds, the thought of being older (not the older but the other things that come with it) and not having my folks, the world having changed radically, knowing more people dead than living etc etc all really scares me.



I just wondered how older people cope with these inevitable stages of life?  Especially in a society where age is not respected and all your experience is not always appreciated?



</question>



<answer from>



Bookworm
Member since: 13 May 2007
Total points: 446 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I understand your feelings. When I was 32 turning 50 scared the crap out of me. But this year I turned fifty and its great. I get to spoil and spend the time with my grandchildren that I didnt get to do while raising my own kids. I have so much time to do the things I didnt have the time or the money before. Lots and lots of me time and me and hubby time. As far as losing the people you love, I have the closest relationship Ive ever had with my dad because I treasure and appreciate every moment we have together.  Yes you may get a little slower as you get older but you also start to see the world with new eyes. Im having the time of my life. Im more relaxed and in touch with myself then I ever was in my thirties. And the people in my life dead and living are more precious to me then ever.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks to everyone who took the time to answer.  Was just so heartbroken could not see wood for the trees.  Your words all spoke to me on a deep level and this answer especially - so thanks all again. x



</asker's comment>

<answer>



Age really is just a number.  If you keep a positive outlook and never stop trying to learn and see new and different things you can keep your life exciting to you.  The pain of lost loved one can be painful and life without them is often lonely but even younger generation members feel pain and are lonely at times.  I suppose what I want to tell you is that life is life  I am 56 hopefully not as old as you were directing this question but I find life goes on in the same way.  The things you have to do change, I no longer need to think about school or homework for myself or my children because they are now grown.  I continue to do the things I always enjoyed and have learned that  we can live very full and very happy lives even if we spend a lot of time alone.  Old friends linger and those now gone remain in memory but in learning and doing new things new friends come into your life.  They do not replace the old but they are the friends of now.  If you keep you mind and heart in the now you can live happily at any age, if that is what you want to do.

<question>



I need some advice, from a Mum?



I wish I could write only a small piece here but it's a slightly long story. Ok... my Mum died in November last year, very suddenly, leaving behind myself (21) my sister (24) and my Dad (52). Very soon after she passed, I took on the roles of my Mum, cooking cleaning washing ironing etc, mainly because I knew my Dad and sister wouldn't have a clue what to do. Soon after my college work was failing and I told my Dad I needed some more help, so my sister then took care of the washing and ironing, I still do cooking cleaning shopping pay bills etc. So all was well then my sister moved out, and I done all of my own things, and my Dad's when need be. I got on very well, my house was always clean, washing always up to date etc, and I enjoyed that but hated living alone. I then got a puppy whom I adore and my sister moved home so we decided to share the responsibilities with both the dog and the house. The thing is that now my sister keeps on about how little I do around the house and I am lazy and all this, what she doesn't realise is that she wouldn't eat if it weren't for me, she probably wouldn't have her car, and most certainly wouldn't have a roof over her head as for a long time we had trouble paying bills after Mum's death and I was the one who took care of it. I wish my family could see and appreciate what I do for them, I hardly have any nights out because I have people and a house to look after yet I took a night 'off' last night and went out, and because I havn't gotten dressed my sister thinks I'm lazy, even although I have done the dishes, looked after the puppy, cleaned, and prepared and cooked tea, she has worked for 2 hours and walked the dog so she thinks she has done more, I need some motherly advice... my Mum was the only person my family ever listened to and I desperatly need to say something to make them change, and realise just how much I do, my Dad isn't here just now but doesn't really give a damn anyway, he's too interested in his new girlfriend.. sorry to have gone on so much but it just upsets me so much so that my hair has stopped growing and is about 15% grey... I need some good advice please x



</question>

At the age you and your sister are, you should have had a family dynamic that meant everyone pitched in anyway. Time to teach that dad of yours how to load the dishwasher, lol. You are important too, don't forget that. You should draw up a rota, get them to help, and just say it's to stop the arguments between you because you don't want to argue with them. And split the bills in three, if your sister cant afford to live their, then tough, she needs a new job. ((HUgs))

<answer>



First of all you are all grieving for your mum, each in your own way, even if it doesn't look like that, the process takes around two years before you have acceptance.



It sounds as though your way of dealing with things was to take over from her, keep the house nice and look after the family just as your mum had done, and now you're being taken for granted.

Maybe you can get the family together and work out a fair division of jobs. Tell your dad and sister that it's going to be difficult to take over all the stuff your mum did, but that you all have to work together to keep the family and the home going, for all your sakes. After all, your sister is older, and should take the lead.

A few days away and a chance to recharge your batteries and have some time for yourself would be a good idea. Is there anyone you could visit, or anywhere you can go, just for a short breather?

I can definitely relate to your situation. My parents have basically rejected any notion of even meeting my boyfriend based on the fact that they think that he's not on my level because of his background, etc. Whilst I understand that they only want the best for me, the fact that they place my financial security (which I can take care of myself, thank you very much) as a much higher priority than my happiness is an issue that I am unlikely to ever reconcile with them on. Anyway, enough about me.

Although parents will always try to do what's best for their children, they are human and as such it is possible that they will not always end up doing what actually is best. Of course, you're human as well and may well be missing something they see that isn't right.

Essentially, it is up to you to take into account everyone's perspective and to make your own decision based on what you think is correct. <answer>



I had a friend go through something similar. I know that if the children were in danger the social worker would normally contact family members first and try to find placement for the children. If they don't contact relatives before they take the kids to foster care, what happens is they go to family court and they usually come up with a home plan there. So if they go to court make sure that your aunt knows about it and shows up because they will ask for family members that they can stay with.Try to have as much family support as possible at the court. Maybe even have your aunt get a few character letters to help out. After finding someone willing to take the kids in, they will come to their home to make it is suitable for them to stay there. I think they will also do a background check on everyone who lives in the home. Good luck!



</answer>

<answer>



You have created a monster :)  Just kidding, actually he has been your "baby" till the youngest came along and now he does feel jealous  and left out at times.  Recall he was about 3 when she was born, you were caring for her, feeding her, bathing, and such while he suddenly began to do everything for himself as he was now a "big boy".



Though she is your baby girl, he is your baby boy and should be looked at it in that manner, giving a little special attention to each of them but don't forget to have a little special one on one time with your eldest daughter too.  You could try having some type of special treat snack for them when they come home from school.  Taking one day at a time making that something special for who ever day it is.



It wouldn't hurt to give him a little extra love and treatment too, rather it be coming up and giving him a hug now and then letting him know how much you love him.  A little extra reward for watching over his little sister or playing together in a nice manner.



As a dad of seven 5 girls and 2 boys, having special time for each was very stressful but rewarding in the end.  The fact that he still sees himself as your little boy and wants your attention is a positive thing.  Give him a little extra time now and then, even joking or playing with him with some game or toys.  It helps and no it isn't feeding his behavior it is letting him know that being the "middle child" doesn't mean that he is left out.



Good luck and wish you the best, there are never any easy answers to being a parent.



</answer>

ladybug
Member since: 03 April 2007
Total points: 4135 (Level 4)

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Oh, Sun... I'm sorry to hear things are not going well. Piling too much on a relationship can be so tough. My hubby and I piled a ton on our plate at the same time that we got married. I won't even go into all of that, but it was ugly, believe me! We could e-mail and trade some nasty stuff I'm sure. You are not the only one who has such bad fights.
One thing I've learned is that all bets are off when it comes to family. I mean that you are always more likely to be short or ugly with them than with others BECAUSE they love you and will allow you to get away with more. (Just imagine if you talked to a co-worker like you do your hubby! THAT would get ugly!) The funny thing is that the same allowance is gone when it comes to suffering. Somehow, we never quite believe the severity of their suffering whether it be a cold or something more serious. Maybe we hear their daily complaints, so then when they have something worth complaining about, we aren't as sympathetic as we should be. 
SO....what I'm getting at here is that it might be a very useful thing for your hubby to hear from some source, other than you, what exactly is going on with all the medical problems in your life, recent past and present. (Books, internet research, a doctor, a women who has experienced it or her husband for that matter!) It might open his eyes a bit. It may also help him to REALLY know that there is some THING that is coming between you two, and it is not a lack of your love for him that makes you panic or snap at him. He may know all the facts about your condition. You may have told him a million times that it is the PPD or something else that is causing some of the tension, but it's hard to make your inner self believe it sometimes when the negative is coming from the safest person you know. Does that make sense?

About the panic attacks
When you are both calm, maybe you two could talk about what kind of situations typically bring on the attacks. Then maybe you could come up with a plan for helping ease that tension. Like if you get panicked feelings when you are getting the baby ready to go out because you are thinking of all the possible problems, clothing changes, diaper shortages, formula mixing dilemmas, etc. that could arise, then maybe you could put together bags of stuff for any and every situation to keep in both cars. And it is his job to keep them stocked and calmly brief you of their sufficient contents when you are trying to get ready so that you know it's under control. 
Find something that calms the whole family. My hubby and I love putting the kids in the car and going for a drive or heading to the mall. When we drive, we often just cruise around and look at houses (our area is growing rapidly). When we go to the mall, we take our girl to the playground and then get chocolate chip cookies. That part is routine, and then we just do what seems fun; walk, shop, play some more, eat, people-watch, whatever. It relaxes us. And, aside from the cookies and drink, both activites are free (and priceless).

About making new friends...
it gets harder as you get older. I know this to be very true. My closest friend and I were drifting and then finally had a falling out several months back. I have friends that are shared with my sister or my hubby, but I have no friends left who are mine and mine alone. I miss those. But you must keep yourself open to possibilities and don't get discouraged when they don't come quickly or easily. Adult life has, sadly, less room from growing good, strong freindships than childhood did. Cherish any connection, whether flesh and blood or here on Yahoo, or anywhere for that matter. Even if they are only shortlived.



</answer>

First you need to seek help for yourself, therapy/antidepressants, no matter what people say about them, they have saved me for the most part.Secondly sit down with your husband and do some research with him about postpartum depression and anxiety, make him understand with research what exactly it is that you are going through. Third couples therapy, especially being in a new place, you guys more than ever have to stick together and fight for what you believe in, don't let the bad come between you guys. Instead of raising your voice even though he may be raising his, talk in a normal tone, it will help the fight and help both of you to calm down and talk like adults. People say so many hurtful things in anger, just let it go in one ear and out the other, because if you keep dwelling on this it will only hurt you more than you already are.

<answer>



I went through the same sort of thing, moved to a different country and then had our first son 5 weeks later.  It's tough to have big moves and even tougher with babies.  You need help with your panic attacks, that probably isn't going to get better by itself.  It will be very damaging to all of you if you can't get this under control.  I did a few sessions with a psychologist as I was having some problems with adjustment to being a mother and the move, it helped a lot.

You probably should keep trying the mother's group thing or a toddlers library session or something.  You don't have to find a bosom buddy there but just getting yourself out will help and you will want to have playmates for your son soon.



Try to take a course or join a group for yourself.  It doesn't have to be anything heavy, just something for fun, but something that gets you out and interacting with other people.

Get a phone plan that has a good long distance rate so you can easily keep in touch with your Houston friends and family.



A digital camera makes sharing photos easy.

Remember that this has been a big change for your husband too.  It sounds like he has switched to a different university and if so he is under some stress trying to establish himself in a new environment.  But snapping at each other and calling names is not going to help either of you.  The two of you might need marriage counseling but I really think that the panic attacks need to be addressed first.

<answer>



Wow, this is very similar to what happened to me. I had a high-risk pregnancy; couldn't keep anything down, including water, and wound up on an IV every weekend. The baby wound up premature due to dehydration, and when he was only about 2 or 3 months old we left my home to be nearer my husband's family. I didn't know anyone, my son wound up having colic (24 hour screams) for a year, and he didn't sleep through the night for a year and a half due to other medical problems. My husband and I fought a lot, but our son's medical issues actually wound up making us really connect. When you're up together all night "fighting the good fight," you either come together or you fray apart.

This is the crux of your issue. You really need your husband's support, and he's not providing it to you. You need to ask him at a mellow, unrelated time how he feels about you and the child in his life. You need to explain what's going on with you and explain you need him (he may feel confused and left out, and leaning on him may boost his ego). Also, if your PPD has lasted a while you need a good OB to talk it out with. Ask for recommendations or go online and google some doctors. It sounds to me like you need outside help. My hub and I went it alone, and we felt the effects for so many years we didn't have another kid till the preemie was 9!!! Best wishes.



</answer>

<answer>



Oh dear. You sound like a lot of people I know. You both should go to family counseling. Its frustrating parenting a teenager and its frustrating being a teenager. Counseling can help you both.

From what you have told us, I think there is a good chance that you both have form of ADHD or a similar disorder.There are different types of ADHD. No one has to be hyper to have it. That is just one type.



Were things always like this or only the last couple of years? If its been an ongoing thing, you should check into it. It might have escalated over the last few years but if there has always been the lack of impulse control and the son has always had problems remembering things like taking his towel in for his shower, its very possible.



There is no shame in having it. It is caused by the neurons in your brain misfiring much as a spark plug misfires. Both cause a faulty  connection which results in lack of impulse control and organization, such as not remembering a towel, putting off homework until its too late, etc. with ADHD and stuttering and puttering with your car.

There are many ways to treat it now. Some will work better for you than others. You don't always have to take medications to learn to control it. Each person is different so you need to try what you are comfortable with and go from there.

Read up on it. See if it sounds like it could be you. This site has both an adult ADHD board and one for children. Between them, you should be able to figure out whether or not you should be tested.



Source(s):



http://www.adhdandldsupport.com/index.cg...



</answer>

In fact statistically kids that go to community college first are more prepared to go to a four yr university and do better the second half at the four yr school than the people who started there.

<answer>



There is nothing wrong with getting an education to better yourself.



The child is two (almost three), and will thrive in daycare with other children.

A lot of parents have to work. It is a necessity these days.

The few hours that she spends in daycare will not effect her in a traumatic way.

Even though your mom and the child's father mean well, they are not the ones who are responsible with providing your child a healthy future. The father is obviously not a full time dad, and the grandma is a part-time helping hand. You are her sole provider.

These few years in school will better your life and the life of your child for many years to come.

Do not let them influence your decision. Everyone is allowed to have an opinion, but that does not mean it counts.

The child can be in daycare 5 days a week, and it will not harm her.

She will be fed, taken care of, surrounded by other children, and learning while you learn.

When you have your degree and look back on this, it will seem foolish.

Best wishes

<answer>



Well done for continuing your education!  I know how hard it is, I am currently in my second year at uni and we have five children between us.  You go girl!

As far as I am aware, nursing is a very demanding course and more time consuming than most degrees, so you will need a good care structure in place for your child.

I see no reason why you cannot juggle your daughter and your studies.  Although at times it will be very difficult for you.



On the whole I have fund my lecturers extremely understanding when it comes to children.  At times I have even taken our 9 year old to lectures with me (she drew pictures for the lecturers - how cute is that).  If I have had difficulty getting to classes, so long as I let them know in advance, and I have made up the work there has been no problems at all.  They are educator, and are more than happy to go the extra mile for mature students because we are there to learn, unlike some of the younger students who are there to toss off three years.

As a parent, your daughter will always been your main priority, however, that does not mean you whole life should be suspended until she gets older.  Working hard for what you can achieve is a very important lesson that needs to be taught, far more important that teaching you child to be welfare dependant.

As for your mum, try explaining how important it is to you that you can provide for your child, not just financially, but the life lessons too, ask her for help and support, but make it clear that you want to set a few day in daycare up too, because it is important for your daughter to learn how to socialise.  Perhaps you could ask her to help you vet some daycare centres, get her involved so she feels needed?

Good luck with your studies!!



</answer>

<answer from>



Cool Dude
Member since: 03 March 2007
Total points: 7000 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



It's complicated, but sometimes a parent can momentarily forget that a mature child is their child.  It seems to happen more often with mothers and teenage or young adult sons.  Normally, there is a mental block in place, called a bonding mechanism, that keeps them from feeling that way about their own kids (and vice versa), but if it doesn't work or misfires for a moment, then they can feel the same way they would about anyone else.

I worked with a woman (wife of a famous professional race car driver) who said she got up one night and caught a glance of her hunky 16 year old son in the kitchen in his underpants getting a drink of milk, and in the near dark she didn't recognize him at first and was totally turned on by him!  But as soon as she realized who he was, she went back to not being able to have any such thoughts.



And a girl I was in college with said she had the identical experience with her own younger brother when she went home for Winter Break and passed him in the hallway one night.



Alcohol, depression, and some medications can all temporarily interfere with the mechanism.  It's just biology, not morality.  Try not to dwell on the episode.  If it only happened the once, I doubt if it was deliberate.



</answer>

<answer from>



little monkey
Member since: 11 January 2008
Total points: 2817 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Take every thing one at a time, otherwise it just seems insurmountable.
Cross off your list things that are out of your control - like your mum and dad's problems - I know they worry you, but you actually can't do anything for them except not cause them any extra stress.
Do easy tasks like filling out your personal details on your ucas form so at least you feel like you're achieving something.
Make a list every day of things you need to achieve and try and complete it instead of having it all going through your mind at the same time.

And make sure you take some time out for yourself - talk to a friend, get some exercise, get plenty of sleep etc



</answer>
